RATING: 7
A combination of alot of familiar ideas from other games (modular board, card drafting, route planning) that comes together into a very scalable, accessible game.

The goal is to either be the first to build all of your buildings, or to connect two temples to each other with your buildings.  This leads to alot of tactical blocking, and the occasional surprise quick-ending.

To build, you need to first get your buildings onto your personal board, and then you have to get them onto the main board, plus you need to meet the resource cost of each building.  To get resources, you can either use the resources on the board, or grab resource cards.  There is also the opportunity to gain extra actions if you build carefully.

This is a good game to introduce to beginners, or your wife, as it incorporates alot of familiar concepts, plays very well as a 2 or 4 player (but not so well as a 3er), and has a light feel to it.  Rates about an 8.5 for me as a 2-player.

The game is a measure of luck and then a run for victory as you collect card sets (very Euro) and place 'buildings'. The problem is there are two ways to win - and connecting is the easier of the two, unless someone cuts you off (which is also very easy). Then it is a war of attrition. So all in all, not something I need to own and am not anxious to play again.

RATING: 5
Reasonable but not as good as I was hoping.  It has overtones of Settlers, Durch de Wuste and even Ticket to Ride (making long links and drawing cards as an option - and playing 2 cards if you haven't got the right one is a bit like drawing a loco).  It's low on inter-action though, and you sometimes have to be the one to block to stop someone connecting the temples to gain a win, yet the cost of doing that is often large in terms of where it leaves you.  I found it a high-mediumish brain-burner, but with less fun attached than other such games.  I also found it a bit dry and even arid at times.  Not a bad game, and I much prefer it to Settlers, but it's not as good as the other games I mentioned here.

RATING: 9
This /is/ the second coming of Settlers.  "Second coming" means that it is better than the first!  Seriosuly, this game has all the strengths of Settlers (except for the trading, but it has got everything else), and doesn't have many of its weaknesses (such as dice luck and rich-gets-richer problem).  It has got the simplicity and fast pace of play which La Citta lacks.  The theme is even better than Settlers, with many different types of buildings with different characteristics without the introduction of dozens of exception rules.  Playability-wise, there are more flexibility and more real options during play, while there is also a suitable amount of luck.

RATING: 7.5
I've just recently come into possession of Attika through an auction at a local game store and I feel sorry that I did not own this game earlier. What a wonderful mix of resource/hand management, clever tile placement, luck, strategy, and speedy play. The game is great two player and I have barely begun to grasp some of the strategies that make for a highly competitive game. All of our games have been won by connection so far, but it has become harder and harder each time. I expect that after we become seasoned players, the "play all your buildings" condition will be more often than not the deciding factor.

Still, I love the theme, love the components, love the speedy play. I've played some multi-player, and I cannot attest to the "kingmaking" problem that many see on this site. Although most of the multi-player I have played has been with newer gamers anyway, so I'm sure they wouldn't notice it at all. It's a great game, I'll be playing it for a long time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Attika is a distant cousin of abstract connection games like Twixt and Hex.  One way to win is simply to connect two temples with your pieces.  

To this it adds some most un-abstract mechanics.  Every piece is a building that needs a different combination and number of resources to be placed.  You are drawing buildings in a random order from face-down stacks and drawing resource cards from a face-down pile.  A neat concept is families of buildings, which are built for free if placed in the right order.  Also clever is the expanding playing area; suddenly, that flank you had sealed off is wide open.

So, Attika is a neo-abstract with a simple set of rules and a short playing time.  There's certainly some randomness, but it also presents some tough decisions around timing (what to build now and what to keep in reserve), risk (can Bob really build three next turn and can I leave it to Carol to stop him?) and resource management.  Attika has been a grower for me; it took a while to appreciate its subtle virtues.

In keeping with the game itself, Attika's artwork is simple and almost abstract.  Production quality is fine.  I've had no issues with the rules.

RATING: 6.5
{1 play • 3 player} Probably the highest rating [6.5] it'll get from me... and it'll likely go down with future play.  The decisions are okay, but it's really more of a race than anything else.  It was fun to concentrate on *my* plan, but I had no interest in seeing what resourses my opponents needed to build stuff (mostly 'cause it's too hard to see), so, really, all you do tacticly is block stuff, placement-wise.  Or, in the case of our game, you don't see that I can place three streets and connect... which I did... quite unsatisfying win.  Eh.  The '10' people are nuts. {after 4 more plays} As predicted, my rating has dropped.  Oddly enough, the midgame is more interesting to me now that I'm familiar with the game, but the endings have become even worse - even when I've won, it's been unsatisfying.  You need the connection victory condition for the tension it creates (otherwise there's *really* no game), but the connection win is always anticlimactic, basically because it never seems earned.  I'd be very surprised if the fans of this game were still playing a lot of it a year or two from now. [after a 2-player game] I've bumped my rating back up because it works really well as a 2-player, perhaps the only I'll play from now on.

RATING: 8
Szép játék! A menete is tetszik, mondjuk kedvenc nem lesz, de nem bánom, hogy kipróbáltam. 30 épületünket elsőként kell lehelyezni településekké formálva azokat. Számít, hogy mi mivel szomszédos, és a területen lévő nyersanyag adottságok is fontosak az építkezésnél. Elég egyszerűek a mechanizmusok, a szabály kb. 5 percben elmondható.
Talán túl nagy előny illeti a kezdőt, annak ellenére, hogy a szabály igyekszik kompenzálni.
---
Másfél év után újra előkerült. Nem véletlen mondják sokan, hogy 1 parti alapján nem szabad ítélni. Bár alapból se volt rossz véleményem róla, de most többet is játszottunk, és kifejezetten jó játéknak tűnik! Máris javítottam 1 pontot az értékelésén, de ez akár még feljebb is mehet.
Először is egy átlagos 2 fős parti 20 perc, nagyon pörgős! Ennek ellenére egész komoly dilemmákat nyújt, mondhatni komoly játék, ez így együtt nagyon ritkaság! Emellett a kétféle győzelmi lehetőség nagyon jó csavar, és nagyon kevés akciófajtával sikerült változatossá és izgalmassá tenni a játékot. Szerencse szerepe nem kicsi, de számomra tolerálhatóan adja ezt el a játék. Annyira tetszett, hogy egymás után 5-öt játszottunk, 2 órán belül lement az összes parti... 
---
Tovább javult a pontszám, nagyon élvezetes, és sokszor újrajátszhatónak tűnik...
---
Hárman is kipróbáltuk: katasztrófa! Ennyire királycsináló játékot még nem láttam. Simán lehet olyan helyzet, hogy a harmadik játékos egyszerűen eldönti, hogy ki nyerje meg a kör végén a játékot. CSAKIS KETTESBEN szabad játszani!! Bár a négyest még nem próbáltam, de annyira nem is vágyom rá ezek után:)

RATING: 6
Nice gateway game.  I prefer playing with the unofficial 3 amphoras rule for connecting the shrines.  It still motivates players to connect the shrines but doesn't force people to make self destructive moves.  It prevents new players also from feeling bad that they let everyone down and let someone win.  Even if players didn't block or forgot to do so, the game isn't over and all the players can move on.  
UPDATE (9-30-06):  7 -> 8

9-17-07
Played the game a few more times and the rough edges are showing themselves.  It has considerable downtime.  For two players it isn't bad as you only have to wait for one player, but it can be significant with more players.  Since on a players turn they don't really know what they'll get because the buildings they get is random, you have nothing to do when it is not your turn.  I guess it allows players to talk about other things while they wait for their turn which could be a good thing.  However, we are here to play the game and we should be playing the game.
UPDATE: 8 -> 7

Oct 25, 2007
The game has sat in my shelf for quite a long time.  I have no desire whatsoever to play it.  Due to luck of the draws one can't really plan ahead so the downtime goes way up as more players come into play.  I find the "connect two temple" victory condition to be "broken" as it almost always places a player in a position to block the person about to win but shoots himself in the foot at the same time.  Thus I don't want to play this game with more than 2 players if not using the 3 amphora variant.  In 2-player games the "connect two temple" victory condition works very well.  However, as a 2 player game it is not that exciting.  I would actually say boring and frustrating.  You can't really plan ahead since you don't know what you are pulling off of the tiles.  This is a mediocre game and you can pass this game without missing anything.
Update: 7 -> 6

RATING: 3
Played once as a 4-player game with the rules explained to me.  I guess I should elaborate on why this game did not win me over.  For starters, in a 4-player game, someone needs to sacrifice their own position in order to be the blocker of a game winning connection.  Second, I dislike the 4 draw stacks.  Athens was on the very bottom of my black draw stack.  As expected, this makes it really difficult to play efficiently, so a lot of my tiles were placed on my player mat instead of on the board.  Third, I dislike drawing cards.  One player never drew a Forrest card, which limited his ability to play buildings.  Finally, I hate the placement of new board tiles.  You cannot build more than 1 building on the new board tile, unless you have an extra action, but then you still have to draw a building randomly from the draw stacks instead of using a building from your player mat.  Perhaps this game is better with fewer players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[COLOR=#959595][SIZE=7]Deutscher Spiele Preis 4th Place 2004[/SIZE][/COLOR]

[i]Posted: 4/21/06, Rated: 8[/i]
{2P:9, 3P:8, 4P:???}

[i]Attika[/i] is game that is easy to learn, looks great, has intriguing pieces, and a veritable "tech tree" of building relationships that closely resemble a Real-Time Strategy (RTS) game on the PC.  All of these are good attributes.

The 2p game is fantastic.  The 3p game is a bit more hectic, and in some cases play feels a bit forced: are you going to do anything about that other person or do I have to do it?  Minus one point for the drop-off in play.

Ideally, you spend your last card to play your winning tile.  That's a mechanism that I have enjoyed in [i][thing=8217]San Juan[/thing][/i] and in [i][thing=9217]Saint Petersburg[/thing][/i].  Using cards to represent the different resources is a mechanic that I really like.  It worked well for [i][thing=13]The Settlers of Catan[/thing][/i] and for [i][thing=6249]Alhambra[/thing][/i], and it works well here, too.

The welcome frustration of "I wish I could do one more thing" is somewhat diluted by the implementation of "amphoras:" these are basically extra action tokens that can be earned during the game.  One one occasion, my opponent didn't draw the tile that he needed to connect two temples and win the game.  I thought "Whew!  I dodged a bullet!"  Then he played an amphora, and won with the extra move.  Maddening when it happens to you, and exhilarating when you do it to someone else.

Most of the time, [i]Attika[/i] is a wonderful game.  Yet there are still occasions where you can't get ahead of the game due to unlucky tile draws.  If the occasional bout of rampant bad luck doesn't deter you, then you should give [i]Attika[/i] a try. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
p: Technically this should be a game that swims comfortably at the high end of my ratings, as it combines many ingredients that usually rub that sweet spot of mine. But I find it hard to rate it any higher as mediocre, as most of the times it bores me to tears.

Someone suggested raiding another box for cubes to help keep easy and more visible track of progress on players' boards, and that alone improved enjoyabality immensely. So with a major annoyance fixed, the richness of the game should come flowing through, right? Nope. Instead the one feeling I can't shake is that despite all the random draws, instead of a opening up a tantalizing variety of tight options, my turn seems to be entirely predetermined by what has been left by my neighbour on the left, and what I certainly don't want to leave for the player on my right. If someone with least to lose skips their "duty" to keep the leading player in check, it will inevitably mean that someone trailing even more can kiss their game goodbye when they take that task. That aspect is present to some degree in most games, but here it can strangle all the fun from game-play.

Pity, it looked so good.

RATING: 7
Interesting game this, you can add parts of the central board as you go along, you compete for the best places to build improvements that are taken from a Carcassonne-esque blind tile draw. You have different resource cards which you can spend to build things like in settlers and there's a couple of ways to win - either linking 2 shrines together or getting all your buildings out on the board first. The clever way that sets of buildings can be played sequentially adjacent to one another for free means that the game can move and change rapidly. Worth quite a bit of thought, this one!

RATING: 8
Attika is a tile-based hex game. Players are attempting to spread their colonies across a Greek island, using resource cards as well as resources printed on the board. The game is a race to do one of two things - either join two temples on either side of the board with your tiles, or get all of your tiles in play.

Attika has a distinct, creative board set-up, makes good use of its theme, and allows solid strategy and tactics in its play.

I reccomend this game to players who enjoy thinking games that still allow for lucky breaks.

RATING: 5
I'm holding off on writing a review until I've played this with a more varied number of people.  However, I love this game.  I've played this with my wife 3 or 4 times now and every game is close.  It is quick to play and quick to teach, There is luck in the selection of tiles.  There is a significant screw-you factor.  There is no in-game text, so it is likely that it will play well with kids.  The only thing is that it doesn't look like there is any deep strategy or long-term tactics.  However, I think this game will get out on the table a whole lot.

Just played this with 4 for the first time and it played well.  In our first game, someone snuck in and positioned himself for an easy win before anyone noticed.  After just the one play, I think this will scale well.  Again: little strategic depth but great fun, quick to teach, and quick to play ( I think our game took about 30 minutes).

Still think it's a good game for non-gamers -- easy to teach, engaging, lit bit of strategy, lots of interaction, and quick to play.  Probably won't ask for it if playing with gamers but will suggest it if playing with non-gamers.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a race game of sorts that is mostly about efficiency.  The more efficient you are in spending your resources, the faster you are going to be able to connect the Oracles and win.  Of course, if you wait until you have the buildings out that allow efficient building, then you've allowed your opponent(s) to build unopposed.  This is the heart of the decision making in the game: build inefficiently now in order to seize the best positions and block your opponent(s), or take actions that allow you to build more efficiently later.  

The kicker here is that the actions that allow you to build more efficiently later refers to drawing buildings randomly from your stack.  Most buildings are free if you have the correct pre-reqs built, so it's important to get those pre-reqs out.  But you don't know where those buildings are in your randomly ordered stack.  The order that your buildings become available to you has a huge impact on the game, and is entirely random. 

There are actions you can take to mitigate the randomness - you can always draw resources instead, which allows you to build whatever you want.  So you're not completely out of the game if your stack turns out to be ordered the "wrong" way.  Still, I can't help but feel that the game gives the perception that it is decided by the building draw, which makes it feel unsatisfying.

RATING: 7.5
Need good planning to maximize your development in this challenging tile laying game. All players can take different approaches to building and come out very close. UPDATE: Very tight resource management (cards) with area control strategy (placing buildings) makes this interesting with either 3 or 4 players. RATING: 6
Updated 8/17/10.  

It's official: I'm burned out on Attika.  People seem to love it, and I did too for the first ten plays or so.  But, like most short, light games, I've run out of depths to plumb.   

My gaming group likes to play Attika regularly, as it's arguably the best game in my collection that can be played in 30-60 minutes while waiting for other players to arrive.  The gameplay itself is simple, but getting to the core of the game takes some real thought.  Unfortunately, where I had once viewed that needed thought as strategic depth, I've come to realize that it's primarily a confluence of draw luck and the fact that I'm just mediocre at the game.   

The game is dry, which I'm okay with, and the artwork is attractive, if simple (probably too simple).  I think some of my opponents found it a bit boring thematically.  It would be nice if there were something more of a tie-in to the ancient civilization theme, which I typically adore.  Why some of the minor industry groups could not have produced a resource when completed, I'll never understand.   

The primary distinction between a low 7 and a high 6 is the frequency with which I'm drawn to play a game.  For a 30-minute semi-abstract, Attika managed to hold my interest fairly well. I'm at the stage, however, where I'd rather explore Samurai against an equally skilled opponent than continue to play the same soulless game of Attika again and again.  

Despite the ratings drop due to its shallowness, it is still a very good short game.  I recommend it mostly as a two or three player game (the four-player is fine, but you feel a little out of control) for groups or couples that like some challenge and spatial interaction but that don't want to be weighed down with a brain-burner.

RATING: 7
The rules for this game are relatively simple, but it can suffer from analysis paralysis when people try to figure out which of their buildings they can place and which buildings they already have down on the board.

I think most (if not all) my plays of this have been as a 3-player game.  (This is probably at least partly because the store copy we often use is missing some red tiles.)  One of the complaints I've heard and agree with about this as a 3-player game is that there's a strong disincentive for player B to stop player A from completing a path between temples, because player C benefits from the block but player B (usually) has to spend extra resources to make the unwieldy block.

Explaining the possible actions in a turn is...strangely difficult.  You can draw 2 OR build 3; if you draw, you may immediately build drawn tiles (but NOT tiles already on the player board).  Any unused actions can be converted to drawing cards.  Each time I've taught this (or I've watched it taught), someone's had trouble with the concept, and I'm not sure why.

RATING: 8
Very nice tile laying semi-abstract that plays well both 2p and multiplayer. What keeps it from getting an even higher rating is that topdecking can make or break equal players, especially on 2p where bash the leader is not an option. Still, a comfortable keeper.

---

+ simple rules
+ clean rulebook
+ temple connecting threat
+ force-in-being threat (holding cards in your hand, keeping a full stack of roads on your mat)
+ space vs resource management creates a good tension
+ blockage and screwage
+ tile expansion timing decision
+ paying attention to your opponent's mat is a must (and easy to do)

- a bit theme-braking (abstract), since the city states can be all over the place
- lack of the draw is a bit too high for the weight of the game, and not easy to mitigate

 What pushes this game to a 10 you ask??..... 3 things

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Despite my initial hesitation about not buying this game it did eventually find its way into my collection: when it is on offer at a sale with 70% off its normal retail price, who can resist. The first games I played were not very succesful because of the fact that there are no markers provided to mark the buildings with you already placed, so I cannibalised another game for wooden discs and [i]boy[/i] did that improve matters. It is still annoying that the board itself isn't very clear so that looking for that particular building still takes too much time, but at least you can immediately see which buildings are nicely 'in line' for free placement. I'll definitely order a custom set of markers to make for a permanent addition.

As for the game itself: I find it's growing on me, which is quite unusual given my initial reservations. As with Marcel-André's other big box game I own (Taluva), the devil seems to be in the fact that there are multiple distinct ways to win this game. Fast placement can secure a victory because of connectivity; slow placement can block such a victory from occurring. I'm not quite sure yet how the luck of the draw affects your game, although it goes without saying that a really good draw will have you walk away with the game easily. (This can only be avoided by the other players actively conspiring against you, and this is rare.) There is indeed a 'newbie on the right'-syndrome with this game, but once the newbie gains some experience, Attika definitely becomes interesting and worthwhile fare.

[b]Update[/b] I think I was too forgiving when I wrote the above. Two problems are standing in the way of my everlasting appreciation and Attika's rise to greatness. One is the luck of the draw. It affects everyone, of course, but it certainly helps if, [i]coincidentally[/i], buildings appear in the correct logical order more often than not. It saves you the trouble of going through the actual building action. At the very least it gives you presence on the board, it takes away resources from your opponents, it forces them to be a little more defensive. Attika offers nothing in the way of catching up here. The second thing is that between seasoned players, temple connections are not really a viable [i]strategy[/i] to win. Oh, I'll happily admit that there is always the [i]threat[/i] as to force people to make a few defensive moves, but apart from lack of cards and/or buildings it's not a [i]strategy[/i] you can count on from the outset to win you a game. It's too easy to block if played agressively. That means Attika doesn't really have two viable roads to emerge victorious: the game has just one, plus a sneaky elephant path. If there is just one viable road, then it doesn't seem particularly fair that players are exposed to random draws as they are. And that realisation is a darned shame. Had Attika truly offered two separate and viable roads, and less influence from the random draw, then the tension between the two roads would have created quite an interesting title. As it stands now, I'm not sure that the game is as interesting as I thought...think... it is. Rating downgraded by 1 point as a consequence.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A clean and clever if dry design. The conflict between immediately building at the expense of continuity or cards, or biding your time and losing actions, offers some pleasantly tense decisions. There is a significant luck factor at work here in the order of tile draws, but  tactical flexibility and aggressive land management in particular can usually compensate. In fact, I find the latter the key element to consistent victory in this game. Also, it works best with two players; the instant win condition ensures that multiplayer games require an unsatisfying number of  right-player blocking maneuvers ("Kill Dr. Lucky syndrome"). 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Components: Simple, attractive artwork on the tiles, cards, and tokens. Could only be improved by adding translucent tokens of some sort to cover up those structures already built, to make it more obvious what lurks in the draw piles. It would also be nice to have some symbol on the tokens to indicate their family of structures; it is difficult to verify on the board whether all of one family have been built in proximity to each other.

Experience: Plays quickly. There is a high degree of luck, hoping to draw useful tokens or cards, but it's satisfying to build up efficient chains of structures and to utilize the resources on the board. This game is about efficiency, efficiency, efficiency. And it can be ruthless as we cut each other off.

First Played: 2004-04-28
Last Played: 2013-10-12

2 players: 4
3 players: 2

RATING: 10
Magnificent game.  Excellent balancing of resource management, land management, and defense.  Multiple aspects to this game must be mastered to be successful.  Works well with two, three and four players.  While there is some advantage to going first in a two-player game, the additional resources makes up for differences in a game with more than two.  The two-player game is a superior competition between two highly skilled players.  More than two players is enjoyable as well.  I would highly recommend this game to anyone but especially anyone who likes Settlers of Catan without the luck of the dice factor.  It is one of my all-time favorites.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The first player to build a chain of buildings connecting two shrines wins. Alternatively, building all 30 buildings is also a win condition.

Building requires resources which are on the modular game board as well as in your hand of cards. As the board grows during the game, more resource spaces become available.

With some patience and planning, many buildings can be built without resources, as long as they are adjacent to their "base" building. This can form chains of free buildings which can in turn yield extra turn tokens.

All in all, a nice mostly tactical skirmish to try to outmaneuver or outbuild the other player. Not nearly as heavy as I was expecting, though... two-player games can easily be only 30 minutes.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Players play Corinth, Sparta, Athens or Thebes, and are in a race to be the first to either connect two shrines with a chain of their buildings, or be the first to complete all their 30 buildings.  Buildings are either drafted from stacks and built immediately, or held on a playing mat for later consideration.  Builds from the playing mat take a separate turn (ie. slower) but allow for more economical planning, which could end up being faster.  Very simple mechanics, tough decisons about timing, space on the playing area, and resources.  Interesting game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I wasn't sure I liked this game at first. The "connection victory" is all too easy to achieve when new players are facing off with one another and not playing good defense. And generally I think that if a game is going to fall apart if players don't behave within certain "guidelines," those might as well be the rules. (Taj Mahal and Fifth Avenue being prime examples of games that are weak if players don't use them as intended by the designers.)

And yet, if you can get over this hurdle, Attika emerges as a very satisfying game. A little bit of luck (but not too much), a nice ability to plan ahead, but also the potential to have to reformulate those plans when your opponents butt up against you and interfere.

RATING: 8
I love building games & connection games -- Attika is a nice blend of the two. Most of the games I've played have ended in very tight games, whether with two or three (I've yet to play with four.) A quick tip -- use glass drops to record on your mat which buildings you've built. For those builings you built that don't touch the others in the group use a different colored drop. This helps keep track of completed groups -- would hate to miss out on getting an amphora, right?

RATING: 8
Such a simple idea, but beautifully executed, which results in a very satisfying game that is both quick to learn and deep enough to provide good replay value.

Pros: 
Very high quality components (thick card / detailed artwork);
Simple rules;
Can play 2 x 2 player games simultaneously;

Cons:
Not easy to win if all your buildings come out in the 'wrong' order;
You need to provide your own markers to cover the building spots on your player mat, after the buildings have been built (unless you have an incredible memory.)

Highly recommended for all levels of gamers.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I played &amp; bought this at Essen and hugely enjoyed it. The placement rules appear simple (but we had some questions about the sequence). The game turns on the decreasing resources. As tiles go down covering available resources, players must use more cards to complete their city. So getting your road tiles down early seems important. When you clear a stack of tiles, you place a new land &#039;hex&#039;. So you must have resource cards in your hand to maximise this opportunity, otherwise the other players simply exploit the fresh land. It seems timing matters a lot, but also card-counting skills too. All the tiles are illustrated on your city sheet, all the resources needed are shown, so you should be able to work out what you still need &amp; how to be efficient. I was stuck at the end because I lacked any water. Brilliant quick game, a must buy and a hit at the show. I got mine for 18 euro.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Played 30+ times with 2, 3, and 4 players for 17+ hours.

The initial buzz was that it was like/better than Settlers of Catan. The similarity is in the resource cards and paying to place down, but it is very much its own game as you seek to exploit resources on the board as well as combinations of free placements. I had only been playing this as a gateway game, and found it pleasant, but not terribly interesting. Then I taught it to a guy who is a real gamer: suddenly Attika has taken on new life. This game can be played in several style: quick, sleek, slapdown as you try and get cheaper building down for free on a board with a lot of visible resources; slower, more methodical setting up building-set combos to limit expenditure; cheap buildings to make a chain to go for a connection win, etc. itself isn't much more than the sum of its parts. Where I once thought it was a pleasant and mildly competitive tile placement game that fills a nice spot in my collection, I now find that amongst stronger players, is quite the game.

Rating history: 4,7...8,7,8...9...8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Ok, there's only 2 things I can do during my turn: draw or build.  So how come it is so agonizing to actually choose between the two?
Simple mechanics with a familiar ressource management conendrum.  Plays fast and provides lots of difficult choices.

Update: Having played more recently (BSW mostly), I realize that there are a number of set options which you must take.  Since the order of drawing will have a major impact on the possible building actions, randomness rears its ugly head in an annoying fashion.  Ex: a quick way to yield a connection victory (granted it's not easy), is to have all 4 roads available with a few bonus turns in hand.  That is only possible if the right tiles are drawn.  I'm not saying I wouldn't play again, I just feel that there is probably a recipe which will yield a better chance of winning given a minimum luck.  This has to affect replay in the long run.

At Storage House
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Received 1/13/04 from Game Surplus. Have now played it over 90 times F-T-T and 60 times on BSW. I love it more each time I play it. This one has the marks of a classic: Simplistic play, agonizing choices, relatively short game length, and strategy! Did I say I love this one? 
2007 -  I am now in my 3rd year of playing this one - and it has not lost any of its glamor for me. Still the best game I own. I now consider it my favorite game, edging out Euphrat & Tigris which reigned as my favorite for 7  years.  Tons of angst. 
Tonight (6/18/07) we played another nail biter. I had 12 buildings left, Ken only 6, and Brent 8. Ken placed two buildings leaving him only 4. I placed 6 on one turn (using 3 amphorae all collected on the that turn). That left me with 6 to Ken's 4. Then Ken placed 2 more buildings leaving him only 2! I placed 3, leaving me 3, but more importantly, blocking up the only spot on the board Ken could place one of his last buildings. On his turn he could only place one. Ken, having no cards, and no now unable to place his last building, had to draw cards. I then placed my final 3 buildings to win! Thus, I placed twelve buildings in three turns! Brent meanwhile, was down to his last building and would have won had I not been able to place my buildings. Ken effectively finished 3rd! What a game!
2009 - Still at the top of my list despite all the greats that have come out in recent times: Dominion, Race for the Galaxy, Small World, Agricola, Le Havre, Endeavor, Automobile, etc. 
2010 - Still number one, but seldom played anymore with so many games to play and teach newcomers. 
2011 - Got to play it again in November and it reminded me why I love this game. It holds total fascination for me. I love its design and the strategic and tactical elements. This is very much like a card game, and you have to know how and when to play your cards (discs actually). This is a game of timing and space. IMO the luck of drawing is no more than in any card game. It's how you use the cards and the timing of their use that wins games. Still my all-time favorite game. 
2016 - Still playing it. Still loving it. Now my most played game, having surpassed Race for the Galaxy. While RftG has lost some of its appeal, this one has stayed at/near the top of my all-time favorites.

RATING: 8
Attika is fantastic!  It certainly has a lot of smarts behind it; Merkle is a fantastic designer and this game doesn't feel like any other tile-placing game I know of. There are lots of things to consider in the game: there is a heavy dose risk taking, great interaction and screw-your-opponent mechanics, and simple design.  I like it as a 2- to 3- player game.  I wonder if I would tire of it after many plays, but until then Attika is one of the nicest tile placement games around!

Attika is an interesting tile placement/area enclosure game.  Although most games of Attika afford you the ability to balance the luck, some outings are throw aways simply because one player has amazing draws while the other is battling their tiles.  I haven't played enough times to consider if there are any viable strategies to counter the tile draws, but because of this inconsistency I can't give Attika a higher rating.  The quick playing time and simplicity of the rules makes this an easy sell for non-gamers who want a little more conflict in a game.  It is this accessability that makes Attika a seven for me, however I am not sure how much this will rise.  

UPDATE:  After a good amount of plays I will state that I find Attika to be a solid seven.  There is always the problem with Attika that it never seems to offer much diversity in experience.  All games feel very similar.  However, with that said the game it offers is solid and enjoyable.  Not much possibility for movement.

RATING: 10
Simple systems interact to create complex, interesting decisions.  The pleasant, easy rules disguise a surprising amount of depth and intricacy.  Each game plays differently due to the number of variables.  Some players have complained about situations in which someone must block a winning move - but everyone would prefer that someone else perform the block. I'm not bothered by this, but those who are bothered might prefer to eliminate the problem by only playing head-to-head games.  Personally, I like it equally well with 2 or 3 players, but would usually choose something else with 4.  I've heard repeatedly here on BGG the accusation that Attika was a flash in the pan, and that it played out after a few plays. I can't speak for anyone else, but for me that couldn't be further from the truth.  I'm still extremely enthusiastic about it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is a reasonably short game -- about an hour in length.  It starts slow, but it ends in a big hurry.  A typical game end is that a player will play all 4 of their roads to connect to two temples.  The first road costs 5 cards minus the number of resource symbols on the neighbouring spaces, but the next 3 roads are free (if adjacent).  So, if you have one amphora, you can play all 4 roads, then win an amphora for completing the road group, then use that amphora to play another building.  That's 5 building tiles in one turn, which can be a surprise attack to win the game.

There are several things to balance here -- drawing your building tiles on your player board so that you can plan to play them in long chains, vs. playing them immediately on the game board as soon as you get them to claim space quickly on the board; collecting resource cards to allow you to play anywhere on the board; and blocking other players' expansion.

The one major thing that is missing from the game is a set of chits to indicate which of the buildings on your player board have already been built (and are already on the game board).  This helps you realize when you have a parent building that will allow you to play a child building for free, and also allows you to realize how close you are to collecting an amphora for building a whole group of buildings.  It also helps other players see these things too -- otherwise, there's way too much to keep track of in this game!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Attika is deeply refreshing. It presents itself as an interesting variation on many things that might otherwise seem like a trope in gaming at this point in time. As both an abstract connection game, a resource conversion/optimization game with hand management, this unholy union presents itself as something relatively unique yet relatively strong in the rules-to-depth ratio. The fact that is it both, yet neither, simultaneously might also have been part of the inspiration for the author to present two competing sets of victory conditions which fit both typical styles of games (i.e., win by connecting the ends & be the first to optimize your resources to build all the buildings, or 30 VP if you prefer thinking about it that way). Regardless, optimal building patterns can help you get closer to building a path across, and coming remotely close to achieving the connection certainly forces your opponent to really lock it down through what often is sub-optimal play of resources.

All in all, with a clever, short, and straight-forward enough rules, a solid production which includes modular boards, I can't see myself growing too tired of this game quickly.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Better than the naysayers would have you believe, but not as good as the hype. I'd need to play it again to firm up my opinion. First play was sort of frustrating, but the good kind of frustrating.

RATING: 7.5
The multi-hex tiles reminded me at once of Taluva, but this game has more going on in conflict and competition, though perhaps less in beauty.

Attika is a 2-4 player game where players utilize resource cards, board resources (on randomly placed starting tiles), and wise placement of building tiles to create a city state. Victory is obtained by linking (2) temples on the crowded boards, or by completing all 30 buildings before their opponents.

There is a very nice balance between resource management (building tiles, resource cards), and expansion opportunity (placing buildings- sometimes for free, completing sectors, placing bonus terrain tiles).

The box itself is lovely, and the Building tiles are nice and solid; the resources are slightly artistic- enough to break some of the resemblence to Catan; the map tiles are very plain, but functional.

Because the assembled maps are small and fill quickly, blocking seems a major strategy, at least to prevent an easy win by connection.  Choices abound, at least until the end when the maps are mostly static and fairly full, but the play is simple enough not to drag, and the game seems well balanced for 2, 3 or 4 players.

This is even more fun to play than Taluva, and I'm really glad I could finally grab a copy. Starting rating is a 7.5, based on 3 solo plays.

RATING: 6
Enjoyed this game, nicely put together and really pleasing to look at (even the box cover is beautiful!).

Game involves building a variety of structures on the game board, which all require different resources in order to construct.  The big twist being that all of the available buildings are in families, where the 'child' building can be built adjacent to it's 'parent' without cost.  Therefore the main tactic is trying to build the parent buildings in such a way that you can simply place all the children down at the end of the game without challenge.  Simple premise but made interesting with all the players vying for space and resources on the board.

My only gripe is that it is a little easy to block the other player's route to (one of) the victory conditions, and it can happen pretty much right at the start of the game.

RATING: 8
Definitely not the "second coming" of Settlers of Catan that some people think it is but it's still very fun.  It combines some elements of Settlers (buying buildings with different resource types) and Carcassonne (placing tiles/buildings) but it's a much different game from both.  For the most part, the game plays somewhat quickly but it occasionally suffers from "analysis paralysis" as there's lots of potential moves you can make in a turn.  The components (especially the cards) are of very good quality, although the player boards are a little too thin.

RATING: 7.5
Acquired 2010-05-22
Sold 2012-01-06
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
2017-07-31

Initial Rating: 6.0 (June 2017)

Attika has been one of the games I most wanted to play since I began playing boardgames in early 2010, so I was expecting a great game and it wasn’t that great, still I kind of like it.

The rules of the game are very easy to teach, you can do it under 10 minutes.

The theme is ok, but you don’t feel it through the game.

The games components and art is ok.

Best with 2 players or 3 fast players although is seems it can have some king making issues with 3 or 4 players.

The game´s decisions aren’t that complex. You have two main actions .You build a building from your board or you draw more buildings. Instead of doing any of these actions you can get more cards to build later. The goal of the game is to build all your buildings or to connect two sanctuaries together. Sometimes you need to block your opponent, but I don’t feel the game´s decisions are hard to process and that’s a shame, I was expecting a heavier game.

Luck of the draw can be high as you can build for free some building if you build its prerequisite at the same time. There is also luck of the draw with the cards. I’m still unsure how I feel about this. I need more play to verify but my gut feeling says it will be an issue for me.

Bottom line, I somewhat like Attika. I was expecting a heavier game but it´s decisions are rather simple. Luck of the draw (tiles and cards) can be high and I dislike that. It is mostly a tactics game, planning long time can to be hard to do.

RATING: 7
MG
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Players create four shuffled stacks of tiles and each turn can draw two from any stack or build tiles that were previously placed on their player boards. When you draw, you can place directly to the main board or, if you can't afford the cost or want to wait, you can play it to your player board. The main board consists of tiles that consist of 7 build spaces, three of which depict resources. The number of starting tiles depends on the number of players. Every tile has a cost in resources to build which can be paid by a combination of playing in proximity to the resources on the board and by cards played from your hand. However, the player boards depict groupings of buildings with a relational flow. If you can build adjacent to an already constructed building that is previously connected by the depicted flow, you can build it for free. A key piece to getting ahead in this game is arranging your tile draws to take advantage of as many free builds as possible. The first player to connect two temples (which start on opposite sides) or place all of their tiles wins.

The tile draws create a fair amount of randomness and it is possible for a good shuffle to make it relatively easy to build a lot of free buildings, which would be tough to beat. For the most part, though, this is a game about assessing the best move each time you draw and is very tactical. It is enjoyable. It's the kind of game I think I don't really need, but more than one geekbuddy has said they got rid of it only to regret it and pick it up again later. I'd better be sure before I make that choice!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
Interesting game.  But, I can't quite yet suss out the balance between skill and luck.  Luck seems pretty high, with the building draws and card draws.  7.5 for right now, but if luck goes up, rating might go down, and vice versa.  I like a good balance between the two, but I'm not sure yet if this game has that balance where I like it...

UPDATE: after a few more plays at different playing numbers, upgrading from 7.5 to 8.5.  Luck is definitely there, but not overly so, not too much.  The game scales really well among different players, with the strategy changing a lot depending on player number.  There's a lot going on under the deceptively simple surface.  For example, in 4 players - you can start strategically stockpiling buildings, or you can jump into the landscape and take advantage of the terrain tiles.  You can start trying to connect shrines or just build based on terrain.  Do you do terrain-based building or block others from their prime building spots?  There's all kinds of blocking decisions going on, and players have to either take it on themselves to block or block others.  Then the tile addition decision adds a whole other layer.  Really nice game.

RATING: 6
This is the first game that I claim to be "overrated."

In my two-player game, there was a lack of tension and no real feel of urgency and very little player iteraction.

This game seems to require a small amount of cognitivie thought as it is basically: draw, play, draw, play.  And I felt as though I won because I drew my tiles in a better sequence than my opponent.

If you want the exact opposite (in a 2-player game), that is, a game with interaction, strategy, and tension, stick with YINSH or Lord of the Rings: The Confrontation, or Carcassonne: The Castle.

I'm not saying this isn't a good game, it's just not gamey enough, didn't require much thought.

I KNEW I was going to win in two rounds, it's that kind of game.  You can openly see who's going to win because all of the game components are in plain sight, with the exception of the resource cards which are used to pay for the buildings, but with a 2:1 trade ratio on your resource cards, you can still get an idea of whether or not they'll be able to construct their buildings.  And "multiple" victory conditions, puh-lease.  It's nice to know that they are there, but your 2-player opponent would have to be passed out in order for you to connect your shrine with theirs, though I did try.  It adds a nice defensive mechanic, and you will attempt it (just for fun, since it is about the only hard part in the game), otherwise it's just a race.  Now that I think about it, the shrine-to-shrine race is the only tension in the whole game; the build all 30 buildings race is boring.


And when I did win, it didn't feel like a big deal.  I didn't feel like I over came overwhelming odds like LOTR: Confrontation, I felt like a runaway leader without built in controls like YINSH's remove a disk when you score mechanism, and I didn't feel like there was any way my opponent could win without the tally up at the end scoring of Carcassonne: The Castle.

A colorful, easy to learn, anti-climatic, low player interaction, solid game.  If my brain was really tired from a long day of work, and I felt like playing a game that plays itself, this one would be an ideal choice.  With that said, I will keep it since it plays well for 2-4 brain-dead players.

RATING: 7
This is almost 2 games- a taut, tense, suspenseful 2-player game, or a too-chaotic, too-many-things-to-watch multiplayer game with a high risk of someone being forced to take a bullet for everyone else just to keep the game going. My wife and I have played this as a 2-player nearly 2 dozen times, and it is invariably a close game, with either player having a chance to win until the last 1-3 turns. There is enormous satisfaction to be found in stringing together a series of effective builds. You are somewhat at the mercy of the tile draws, but it would take a seriously bad draw sequence to make a game unwinnable. I don't ever want to play a 4-player round of this again, but am happy to take it out for a 2-player session anytime.

RATING: 5
This game has generally left me flat. One good thing is that it is short enough to do two rounds right after one another.

Here are my issues.
- The winning condition of connecting two temples seems overpowered. I've won every game I've played by connecting two temples.
- The two actions are "build" or "draw". Except that you can build during the draw action. This is really annoying to teach new players.
- Explaining how you draw cards is also like pulling teeth.
- The amphoras seem like an afterthought. In fact, we tend to forget about them.

Given the complaints above, it really sounds like I hate the game. This is not true. Instead I am indifferent towards it. I have won every game played. Not one of these victories has been very satisfying.

RATING: 7
Though ostensibly an ultra-light civ building game, Attkia is almost an abstract. It's got good quality components, and the style of muted, conservative artwork that has come to be typical of Euros from the '00s.

Gameplay can at times feel like it wants to be longer, which is my way of saying that sometimes it feels like it's all over too quickly. This may be a symptom of playing with 2 players (the only way I've played so far), where a move is likely to either help you or hinder your opponent, but not both. With 3 or 4 players, I expect things become less clear cut, and there would likely be a bit more backing and forthing.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Played a few times including BSW and actual game, with both 2 and 4 

It is true that you are often faced with the decision of preventing a win and crippling yourself significantly, often making it improbable that you can win.  You just have to make those decisions and try talking other people into stepping in now and then as well.  If you all play the martyr equally it works out.  If everyone is leaving it up to you, you're basically deciding if you should let opponent "a" win now, or make the game last longer and probably give the game to opponent "b" or "c".  With the second scenario there is that slim chance you can still pull it off, so people usually do that. I really like this one.  It's satisfying to work on your own empire-building and progress, even if you don't ultimately win the game.

UPDATE: raised from 8 to 9 after more plays.  Love this game and looking forward to adding it to my collection.

This is my favorite amphora game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Attika has been on my playlist since it came out. This has the best elements of Settlers and Carcassonne yet feels completely different than either. You feel the ramifications of every move you make for turns to come - including the 1st one!!  I "get" the strategy behind this game. This is not about placing every tile you draw on the board if you can pay for it, this is about economics! 

Typically, you'll want to place a tile on the board immediately IF:

1. It can be placed but doesn't screw you out of completing a series of tiles for the amphora because it's out of sequence.

2. It can be placed on the board for free or at most a single card.

Otherwise it is probably wiser to place it on the city mat. You want to be very efficient with your resource cards so if to place it immediately it costs you 2 cards, you still have to spend ANOTHER turn to replace those cards. It's often better to put it on the mat and build things in sequence and for very cheap! (That's my strategy in a nutshell!)

For some reason, I tend to do really well at this game too, with a win percentage of about 65% mostly in 4 player games. This, of course, is not due to any spectacular play on my part, but more from the fact the the people I play it with reeaaally suck at it!

I bumped this up to a 10 as I will never turn down a game of it and I find it very enjoyable with every playing. Go and buy this now.

Downgraded to a 9. Don't get me wrong, I still love Attika, but I'm just not singing it's praises as much as the other 10's in my collection. It's really now an "almost" 10 - still fantastic, but just not quite as enthralling as it once was.

RATING: 6
I really like the concept and the idea of it, but unfortunately the game doesn't quite work with me. The game is pure maximisation, and in fact would be a solitaire game if the board wasn't so small (and despite the board size, it almost is solitaire). The maximisation process is hampered by a severe random element (the tiles to be built are drawn randomly, as are the ressources... and with a lesser impact, so is the board itself). Now, some randomness is necessary in a maximisation game, or else it would simply become straight-up chess -- but with Attika, the random element is pushed too far.  

Plus, I tend not to like min-max games.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The verdicts still out on this one.  There is luck involved from the tile draw but I felt it's a game of positioning yourself to take advantage of good tile draws ("buildings" you need).  I felt a lot of tension, which is a good thing, as it's ultimately a game of nickels & dimes, a bad or costly placement here or there (keep in mind you can never move any of the buildings once placed) and you'll lose the game against a worthy opponent.

The 3-4 player game can be frustrating if you need to place in poor spots in order to defend while others watch instead of helping.  So far, best as a two player.  Perhaps providing a bonus in the multi-player game, when 2 shrines are connected, rather than an outright victory would fix this.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
A most excellent tile-laying game.  Players all start with an identical set of tiles, and during the game attempt to either connect two temples with an unbroken line of tiles or place all their tiles on the board.

Each tile requires a specific set of resources to place on the board.  These resources can obtained by placing a tile adjacent to them on the board or payed for by cards.  Additionally, the tiles are broken up into sets; in each set of tiles the tiles are linked together in an order.  If a player can link the tiles together in this order on the board, they can build them for free. 

Adding more interest, most tiles start out upside down in front of the player.  Players can either choose two of the upside-down tiles (either building them on the board or putting them face up in front of them) or they can choose three of the face-up tiles (but they can't mix these).  This leads to very stressful choices.

This game has elegantly simple rules which lead to a complex game system full of stress and fun.

Recommended!

RATING: 6
Attika is a nice euro-style gateway game. I use it to get potential players to have a go at boardgaming, and it definitely works in most cases. There is some "luck of the draw" element involved, but gameplay is still strategic - trying to hinder your opponents and building your own city-state requires a lot of consideration in terms of resource management, optimal building placement and comboing. There is also a bit of kingmaking, but oh well - it isn't a huge issue. All in all, Attika is one of my most played Eurogames.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I'm weak for simple area control mechanisms and yet I missed this little gem. Attika mixes area control, hand management, city building and modular board to create a game full of tension and surprises.

Place your buildings on the board to carve out a territory for yourself and grab the best resources. Manage your geographical cards to keep your expansion options open. Manage your city-state to take advantage of free buildings (when building in the right order) and extra actions (when completing a group of buildings). Not enough space? Then time your depletion of tokens to place new area tiles.

I have only scratched the surface of this simple yet deep game and look forward to many more games.

Update: Sold because I feared that the luck of draw was too decisive and I haven't been able to get it to the table enough to refute this.

RATING: 5
My rating for Attika has increased the more I've played it, and that's a very good sign.  I bought Attika as a two-player game primarily, as I'd heard that it was the best way to play it.  But the fact is that I prefer it with more players because the temple connections are easier and more of a threat.  When that is the case, the games move more quickly and are more fun.

There are still some issues with players being forced to make moves to stop someone else from winning, and who has to do this, but the game plays pretty quickly so I can forgive these small issues.  If the game draws on too long, it can get a little old, but I am very pleasantly surprised at how I've warmed to this, and that bodes very well for the future.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
In ancient Greece, players put buildings on the mapboard where the  cost is paid by terrain or cards in hand. The player mat serves as a temporary holding area for buildings. Bonus turns can be earned if buildings are constructed in a group. First person to put all 30 buildings on the map, or connect two temples is the winner. Some tactics, some being opportunistic to find more space for your buildings while interacting with encroaching opponents. A couple of times, I have seen players accuse each other of not sacrificing themselves to prevent someone from connecting a temple. A little bit of luck depending which of your buildings you draw and in which order. Fairly hyped game from Essen 2003, but it is decent.

RATING: 9
While the randomness of drawing building can be frustrating, the rest of the game has enough planning to balance. Using the board spaces efficiently is vital. It's important to realize that there is a balance between taking the most optimal move from a resource standpoint and making moves that will hinder your opponent's progress. Blocking is important unless you want someone to sneak into a victory. There is still a fair amount of luck involved from the tile pulls, but there is still a fair amount of strategy involved.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.3
It has all the looks of a classic, with a box cover that evokes the games from 15 years ago. Still, it the art doesn't feel old at all. The components are just average.

The rules are very easy to learn and teach, and the gameplay is fluid, but there's not much to do in the time between turns, that's the reason people recommends it as a 2 or 3-player game (this, and the blocking resposibility).

Within these simple rules there are a lot of decisions to make, beyond the most evident ones: Should I build now, or shall I reserve the free builds for an important move? Etc.

Strategy, tactic and luck. But you need adaptability because the situation will change. The game flow is fast, and the game don't last long; the should last less than the advertised 1 hour, much less if you take the short route.

RATING: 8
Playing Attika has a feeling of building your empire even if it is really an abstract. The game has enough "theme" that it doesn't feel too abstract to me. Playing is mostly about optimizing your own resources and making it hard for other(s) to do it but 'Connect two shrines' -sudden death ending condition makes gameplay interesting. Because of it game isn't just minimizing your building expences. Unfortunately this also makes 3 and 4 players games a bit uninteresting if every player isn't on the same level. Then game can easily be decided if less experienced player isn't aware opponent's chances to end the game (or takes unnecessary risks). This can lead into boring end of the game. Therefore Attika is best when played with 2 players.

In 3 o 4 giocatori c'è il rischio che ostacolare il leader provochi indebolimento lasciando troppo spazio agli 'spettatori'. Il gioco premia chi peova a costruire la via giusta (tra i due templi), piuttosto che chi cerca di distruggere. Molte partite si concludono senza collegamento ma solo con la conclusione delle proprie tessere da piazzare.

RATING: 6
Lovely and original modular gameboard tiles are really just the surface of this one. There's plenty going on with two- and three-player games, although four-player doesn't quite add up. The way the game divides itself into little sub-problems is engaging and makes less of a cerebral challenge than at first sight. Sadly the repeatability quotient isn't high: successive games will just not have sufficient difference to be remembered in the long run.

Further Thoughts - As I suspected, Attika is becoming somewhat monotonous in that all games are playing out exactly the same way. Downgraded from a 7 to a 6.

RATING: 7
A good game, probably best with 2.

The positives:
Easy to teach and quick to learn, reasonably fast to play. There is a nice mix of luck and of strategy, and there is plenty to do between turns. The components are good* and the rules are well presented.

The negatives:
It suffers "Kill Dr Lucky Syndrome": players are obligated to prevent the person following them from winning. If a player is inattentive or less experienced they will easily hand the game to the person after them. This can be intimidating to newbies and frustrating to the other players, it can also lead to table-talk and consequent unhappiness.

Colourblind note: the red and green tokens were indistinguishable for me - I have added 5mm self-adhesive stars to the red counters and it's fine.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I think this is an outstanding design, a tile-laying, area control game that's obviously been well thought-out and thoroughly play-tested. A classic Euro--easy to learn, relatively few rules, lots of decisions to make but not overwhelmingly so, and no player elimination. 

The first player to connect his buildings successively between two temples,or, much more likely, to complete the construction of all 30 of his buildings, wins. 

Game plays can be very tense to the finish. Also, the production by Rio Grande is very attractive and the components are well made. 

The play of tiles generates changing terrain factors that can help or hinder building. It also matters in what order buildings are placed, because if done in the correct order it can be done without cost (in cards played) or even create extra free play turns. 

As advertised, "Attika" can be played in about an hour, although time can vary depending on how quickly players decide to move--figure more time for additional players. 

The only thing that holds me back about playing this more often is that it's fairly abstract, and therefore, in my book, a bit dry. But when I feel like playing a more contemplative game, this does the trick. The only reason I don't rate it higher is because I don't play it more often, which tells me something.

I'm not sure why this game is so much fun or why it's so good...other than the mechanics balance and fit so well together it keeps you coming back for more!

RATING: 7
規則:
*根據玩家人數設置版圖跟起始祭壇，資源卡洗勻
*每個人拿一個玩家圖版跟自己顏色的建築板塊，背面黑色的六個洗勻蓋著疊起，其他洗勻蓋著平均分成三疊，每疊最上面的建築板塊打開放到個人圖版對應位置，隨機起始，抽四張資源，照順位每個多一張
*遊戲照順位輪流進行，直到有人放完30個建築或是將兩個祭壇相連勝利，玩家行動二選一
>抽建築  從最多兩個建築，如果有對應資源可以馬上蓋，不能蓋就放到圖板上
>蓋建築 最多從自己圖版蓋三個到主圖版上
*每次抽或蓋的行動可以不做改成抽資源卡，一旦抽卡就不能再做行動，手牌無上限
*蓋建築可以蓋在任何沒有建築跟祭壇的位置上，費用在板塊上，放置位置如果相鄰或上面有對應符號可減免，剩下的手牌出(兩張資源卡可當百搭)，如果照圖版上箭頭方向上方建築已蓋，要蓋下方建築到相鄰上方建築的位置時可免費蓋
*道路要五個任意資源，可用圖版的抵免，之後蓋在道路旁免費
*每一區完全相鄰的建築為一個聚落，每產生一個新的聚落要支付其他自己聚落總數的資源
*如果玩家將自己圖版上同組板塊全部蓋在同一聚落，獲得一個水壺，沒水壺拿就抽一資源卡
*花一個水壺時可以抽一個建物或蓋一個建物也可抽牌，拿到的當回合就可使用
*當四疊建物一疊抽完時可以抽新的地圖板塊拼放，，至少要跟場上相連一邊
******SOLO變體*****
*設置一個三邊長為四個板塊的大三角型圖版，將祭壇放在三個角落，再抽兩個板塊放一旁，可起始少40分來抽板塊自己組合
*個人設置相同，起始資源卡任選兩張
*遊戲目標為相連三個祭壇
*每回合行動為抽一個板塊放自己圖版，然後放一個，每多形成一個聚落要多支付兩張牌
*只有在連到兩個祭壇時才將放一旁的板塊拼湊到主圖版區
*主建築區全蓋完會拿三水瓶，其他1，水瓶只用來記分
*遊戲進行到連線三祭壇或是板塊放完或是不能放
*計分規則
>隨機地圖 40分
>三祭壇連線 30分
>抽最後一個板塊錢完成連線 30分
>每張出的資源卡 -10分
>thebes被完全接觸(或任意城市) +15分
>每個水瓶5分
>所有版塊都放完 30 分
*基礎 70分 100分算高分

RATING: 6.5
I would rate this as a lightish game that takes no more than an hour to play and less if the people involved are experienced players. The mechanics for building are somewhat akin to that of Settlers except that there is no trading to be done and cost are all stated on the building tiles. What is interesting is the placement of the buildings so that it would effectively cut another player off from reaching the next shrine. Thankfully there are ways around that problem as once a player finishes a building stack, they would be able to expand the current map by placing another map tile down. To me, I think this game is highly suitable for 2 players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
This is an ok game that relies heavily on flip luck, and has one big design problem. The general idea of the game is you build a semi-random board, and then players flip a few of their building tiles. The goal of the game is to be the first player to build all their buildings, and there's a cute technology chain where certain buildings when placed next to other buildings get built for free. The problem is to get more buildings, you flip them randomly. This is necessary otherwise there would be "best paths" though the game, but which building flips when is so important to the game, that it hurts the idea that strategy is involved in making your building choices. Not to mention, in order to prevent players from sort of carving out there own areas and not interacting with anyone, the game says there's an alternate way to win by connecting corners with a contiguous chain of your buildings. This is pointless, as there's no rule against having players start their buildings next to these corners, so blocking off corners is trivial to the point of making this rule useless. It's a nice idea for a game, but it just doesn't work.

:nostar: Art and components
:nostar: Innovative mechanics
:nostar: Handles 5 or more players
:nostar: Good with different player counts
:star: Low time between turns
:star: Comfortable complexity
:nostar: Re-playability
:star::nostar::nostar: Enjoyment

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Planning ahead is important, but your turns only consist of 2 or 3 actions so it's not too difficult to juggle everything.  If you make it to an end game with several "extra action markers" you could have to plan a 4-7  action turn all at once, which is a bit of a brain crunch in comparison to the rest of the game.  Don't let the resources fool you.  There's so much low-cost and no-cost building in this game it doesn't have the feel of a building game like Settlers.One can win by connecting their city from one temple to another.  This mechanic mostly serves to force one player to "bite the bullet" and block a player even if it's not their best turn to make (though, it could happen to be).  Overall, a fun, medium weight game with a few mechanics that don't quite fit my personal tastes.

RATING: 8
Nice clean tile laying game. While there is a certain amount of luck based on the sequencing of tiles in the hidden stacks, this can be managed to a great degree by flushing out or reducing to a single tile one or two stacks. By the time you've flushed out a stack, the main board isn't too congested -- but it is getting very close to that point which is a sign of a well designed set of game mechanics.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Easy to learn, middle-weight game. Luck-of-the-draw of the gameboard tiles has the biggest effect on play. Otherwise most of the decisions seemed straightforward. It's necessary to build the 7 tile groups in sequence to take advantage of the free tile placement. Maintaining/limiting free space to allow this for yourself and inhibit it for opponents is critical to the endgame play unless victory is achieved through temple connection.  Can be played from 2 to 4 players. Temple connection should rarely happen if players watch out for large card hands and collected amphora; so it should be a 30 tile placement race.

Update: I was wrong, all the games have ended in temple to temple connections along the edges of the map using only half the pieces. Every player works their own edge and if any effort is made to block another player the result is both players will lose. A bigger map would help a lot.

Update 2: I've now seen a 30 tile winner due to the other 3 players (myself included) not setting up collection of free tiles, amphora, and card hands so that incremental additions were easily blocked. The 30 tile player didn't keep to a single connected area but maintained room for amphora-collecting expansion and free tile placements.

RATING: 9

Will try this one again

RATING: 5.5
To me, Attika is another good example of throwing lots and lots of mechanics into one big pile and making a game out of it that might work in terms of mechanics but is not more than the sum of its parts and I fail to see the overarching concept and ingenious 'designer's handwriting' behind the run-of-the-mill euro mechanics. The basic idea is not bad and almost sounds like a simplified checkers or chess version, a competitive abstract game but it is muddled up some inelegant rules and possible actions which prevents the game to flow and instead every turn feels like a damp squib. I am sure some people will like this game, but it did not hit a nerve with me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
This game was my downfall.

After some neighbors convinced us to play Catan and I learned that board games don't universally suck, I started talking about Catan at work. A coworker overheard me and convinced me to try out Attika with him. When I declared that I liked it and wouldn't mind owning a copy, he told me it was "out of print". That was the turning point in board gaming for me; that was the point when I should have walked away.

And that's exactly what I did. I was resolved to just let it go. Unfortunately, the next day he showed me that someone who lived not too far from me had a copy for sale on "BGG".

I've avoided social networking, blogs and the numerous other products being used to turn our lives into one big reality show, but I figured there was no harm in creating a BGG account just long enough to buy this game. I didn't even bother trying to come up with a clever username. When I went to pick up the game, the seller also gifted me a copy of Babel. Ok, now I own Attika, Babel, and Catan. That's a collection, right? I've got this neat new BGG account, I may as well add my collection to that site and keep my game thoughts on there. I won't start broadcasting my opinions or posting in threads. Just notes to self.

And then an FLGS had a big clearance sale to get rid of some OOP stock. I think I bought 12 games that day. It seemed absurd to own that many games. I kept them in a little pile of shame on the living room floor for a few days. I took a few of the games back, but the proprietor of the shop reminded me this was a sale of OOP games and some of these wouldn't be available again. That was the moment when my wife should have locked me in the closet while she burned every game in the house.

I took back Citadels after reading about how "mean" it was and how your spouse would throw things at you if you played it with her. Then we played that Babel game (against the dire warnings of the Eurowankers) and discovered that we *liked* throwing things at each other and I reacquired Citadels (along with one or two other games). Then the friend who introduced me to Attika started telling me about this Battleline game that was OOP (temporarily, it turns out) but was absolutely fantastic. Calling around, I found a copy and we loved it so much we went on a quest to find more games like it. We never did manage to find another game that inspired such agony and delight in such a small simple package, but along the way we discovered many other things we loved. That was also the first year I didn't replant my garden.

I discovered (or reaffirmed) that I am a completionist and a collector. I confirmed that I have a strong fear of missing out and a weakness for limited time offers. I've known these things for years and have done what little I could to keep my data out of the greedy hands of the marketing firms masquerading as product companies (e.g. Google, Facebook, WalMart). I knew that if I wanted to keep enjoying the simple timeless pleasures of life, I needed to stay away from anything that purposefully preyed upon people with my weaknesses. My garden will never need a product that will go out of print (except that Monsanto likes to buy up heirloom seed companies and "discontinue" them), but my garden does need my time; time I am instead wasting tracking down OOP games and individual cards pulled from boxes to sell as promos at inflated prices; time I waste "researching" (read: throwing money at) overproduced prototypes on Kickstarter (My latest rationalization: "My time is worth $XX/hr so it's cheaper to just buy the game than to spend time researching it"); time I waste trying to pile up shrink wrapped boxes against the wall when my bookshelves no longer have the space.

If I'd know what those first 12 games were going to turn into - if I had any inkling of how deeply I would fall into this trap - I never would have made this BGG account.

And perhaps the most telling part of it all is that I still have never played my copy of Attika.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
There's obviously strategy here, with resource management, board locations, tiles with different costs, etc - but it has an infuriating amount of luck thrown in which seems out of place in a game like this (to me).
The beginning tile draws vastly impact what you can build (and where). As the game progresses, it seems like the amphoras are so critical to winning (against evenly-matched skill players), but obtaining them is dependant on pulling the right tile to finish your section (completely luck-based) that it can be a game-stopper.
It was in my first game, where I drew early and often, more than anyone else, but never got the tile to finish any of the 3 of 4 areas I had set up and planned for.

RATING: 8
Very interesting "make a path" game. The game board is composed of 2-4 interlocking hexes and an equal number of opposing shrines. (The exact number is equal to the number of players.) Make a path between any two of the shrines and win the game. If everyone is on their toes and players block each other properly, this doesn't happen, and the other victory condition kicks in (first to play all their buildings). Buildings cost resources to play, but this cost can be often mitigated (nearby on-board resources can be used to pay some or all of the cost) or eliminated (a "child" building can be freely played next to its parent; e.g., you may play a ship next to your harbor for free). Add to this an additional resource penalty for starting a new settlement (a settlement is any group of connected buildings) and a turn bonus when placing all the tiles in a familial group together and touching. (That's what the amphoras am for!) In practice it's very difficult to completely block out a shrine because each player has as many as four opportunities to add a hex tile and extend the map. I've played this as a 2-player and as a 3-player game, and it scaled very well. I can't see why it wouldn't scale to 4. Enjoyable.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
As a sucker for tableau-building games (such as Glen More and Race for the Galaxy and so on,) I immediately took to this game - even though it's more of an area control game I think. The allure of tableau-building games (for me) is that you produce your own little concoction at the end of the game - which you can admire and take pictures of and stroke affably. Much like the painting which is left on your plate after devouring a beet salad. It turns consumption into a creation somehow. Which is satisfying to the innately productive. The art is also beautiful here.

I do sense a weakness in this game, however, which only time will tell. I feel like its strategy is solvable. Obviously there is a strength given in the extra action bestowed by the "carrots". And obviously drawing tiles and laying them immediately gives an enormous advantage. So it seems the ideal strategy is to draw cards very early and then draw/lay tiles until you are depleted. Then draw cards again. Then deplete. (Never using the lay tiles action except to draw cards.) Then, on your last turn, plan it so you can run out all of your carrots at once, since by then you should know what's left in your piles and can be guaranteed a finish.

Now, the strategy on the board itself can wreck this - but if you look at it this way, the game becomes very similar to Ticket to Ride!! Draw cards, pay for route-building, back and forth. I like this better than TtR, but I feel there is an upper limit to my enjoyment here.

Still, a sweet game!
