RATING: 8
Excellent middleweight tile laying game. Players are trying to use landscape cards to build 30 buildings or connect their cities between two shrines. The landscape provides free resources and players get to build buildings free if they follow the technological tree.

Lots of good decisions to make, a little bluffing, great looking components and very intense competitive gameplay.

RATING: 6
[2012-04-28]
Mielőtt a megjegyzésemhez hozzákezdtem volna megnéztem kinek hány játéka van itt a BGG-n ezzel a játékkal és 100 fölötti számok röpködnek. Nem véletlenül. Ketten rögtön lenyomtunk vele 5 játékot. Tehát gyors játékról van szó, négyen biztosan hosszabb, de nem hiszem, hogy vészesen megnőne a játékidő. A másik dolog, hogy az egyik legtöbbet játszó felhasználó másik kiemelt játéka a Tigris és Eufrátesz és ha jobban belegondolok nagyon hasonló abban a két játék, hogy nagyon kellemes, de érzi az ember, hogy elég sokat számít a szerencse benne. A játékosok épületeket építenek és a folyton épülő pályán úgy próbálják lehelyezni őket, hogy minél kevesebb nyersanyagot kellejen kifizetniük közben. Ezt megtehetik úgy, hogy nyersanyaglelőhelyekre vagy melléjük teszik, illetve úgy, hogy egy fejlődési fát követve a korábbi épület mellé építik. Egyszerű szabályok, gyors játékmenet, többfajta stratégia. Ez utóbbi abból adódik, hogy egyrészt végfeltétel az összes épület lerakása, illetve a játék két legtávolabbi pontjának összekötése. Ez a két cél érdekes, mindig más játékot eredményez. Jó játék az Attika, bármikor kategória, de a szerencsefaktor miatt magas pontszámot elsőre semmiképp nem kap tőlem, illetve van egy olyan érzésem, hogy igazán csak ketten jó.

[2012-08-18]
Hárman egyértelműen fenn áll a veszélye, hogy aki beáldozza magát a másik összekötésének meggátolására, az időt veszít a harmadik féllel szemben. Négyen azért megnézném egyszer, de tipikus kettes játék. Ezen túl van benne nagyon egyértelmű stratégia így ezt játszva túl automatikus a játék és tulajdonképpen a jó lehelyezésen és főleg a szerencsén múlik a végkifejlet. Eggyel rontom a pontszámot, de ettől függetlenül tulajdonképpen bármikor jöhet kategória.

RATING: 8
This is very much a gamer's game, and one that I think puts Casasola Merkle in the top rank of designers. Once again I am impressed with his ability to create a lot of play value with quite simple mechanisms. Unlike many tile-laying games, Attika has straightforward victory conditions, and I like that. I'd rate the game higher if it were a bit faster paced with > 2 players; also the building tiles really need colors or insignia to easily identify them by group. And despite being a very solid game, it somehow lacks the sparkle that makes you want to play it week after week.

RATING: 8.5
I really like this one. At first I thought it too abstract, which I don't like, but it has really grown on me the more I play it. One thing I really love about it is the component quality and the graphic design. Everything is very easy to see and distinguish from everything else, the colors are great, the bits feel good in the hand, and it stores most excellently in the box insert - kudos to whomever was responsible for this! I get very tired of game bits that try to be so thematic that they seem cluttered with graphics or are just plain hard to interpret - none of that here. 

So, players all attempt to build a path of buildings that span from one end of the playing board to another, while blocking each others best efforts to do so. So, yes, there is always blocking in this game, which normally I think is a very uninspiring game mechanic (see Ticket to Ride...) but here it is so intrinsic to the game that you know it will be there almost every step of the way; it becomes more or less invisible to the nerves, like the ever-present ticking of a clock that one becomes desensitized to. There are other, more interesting mechanics in Attika (nothing ground-breaking, mind you) and it all works very smoothly and elegantly, chugging along at a very comfortable pace. As a matter of fact, as I write about this game I am feeling an unmistakable urge to play it - which is a very good sign. There is luck in how the building tiles are drawn, but to me this presents the exact amount of luck appropriate for this type of game, with this type of playing time, and this type of depth.

RATING: 6
Connection game with resource management. Attika is a good 2p tile-laying game with alternate victory conditions and simple-but-satisfying combo/chaining possibilities. Apparently it plays with up to four, but it seems made for two. The theme is meaningless and the components are unremarkable in that old euro way, but the rules are simple and there's a good amount of interaction. Luck of the draw is significant, probably too much for many euro gamers, but given the game's weight and playing time, it doesn't bother me. Underrated and worth seeking out.

Those were my first impressions. Subsequent plays have tempered my enthusiasm. Luck of the draw is more decisive than I'd like, and the alternate victory condition operates too rarely to create much tension. What's left is the admittedly enjoyable combo/chaining, but that's not enough to keep me requesting to play the game when there are so many great 2p games available. Consequently, this one seems destined to hover between a rating of 6 or 7.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

The tactics that can be employed here are manifold and vary a lot depending on the different groups building tiles.

RATING: 6
Attika is nice enough if you play with two. Somewhat inoffensive, nothing spectacular, but all-round pretty decent. With more, it has a huge stop-the-player-to-your-left-from-winning problem. If one player is about to win by connecting two temples, it is usually up to his right neighbour to prevent this. This provides the other players with a free turn, basically. Pretty nasty in an otherwise decent game. But even apart from that, I never found it interesting enough to keep in my collection for too long.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
[RGG 2003 Edition] {Greek, city building, couple; tile-laying, drafting, connection; optimal 2p} Attika is a good tactical Euro game with nice player interaction. It's a connection game where players have to optimize their moves. The game system is fairly simple and fairly fits the theme.

The bits are sturdy but markers should be provided to help players visualize placed buildings on the players' mat.  It is easier and faster to play Attika with makers.

Attika would be better if it allows players formulate a definite strategy. So far, I find this game tactical, instead of strategic, because it has a number of random factors - card draw, tile draw and building draw. Thus, players are usually forced to make tactical move. Nonetheless, players have certain control out of the draw - building draw can somehow be predicted, card draw can be mitigated with 2 cards combo, and a player can control the time of drawing new tile.

Some of my friends are complaining that the shire-connection winning condition is not working; however, I think its mere presence already affects the game plan and increases player interaction. When it comes to a point that a player is going to connect 2 shires, every other player will work to block his path. This increases tension and excitement of the game. Thus, this winning condition is important and has effects.

[Taluva] Taluva seems to be a similar game as Attika (in terms of depth and ways of thinking). It's more tactical, attractive and interactive. I would like to have a play.

[Traded away in Nov 07.]

RATING: 5
This game falls on the wrong side of the Great Eurogame Divide, which I'm becoming increasingly aware I have in my mind.  On the one side are the games where players are equally situated and have a very similar opportunity path at the beginning of the game (Caylus, PR) and on the other side are games where everyone has the same potential but only gets to see a bit of it at a time, thereby introducing a lot of luck into a game that doesn't have enough player interaction to iron that luck out.

Basically luck with little player interaction=bad.

RATING: 7.4
An interesting game that starts as a race game and the key to winning is realising when winning the race (by connecting temples) is no longer possible and knowing when to make the game an efficiency game.

You can attack other people's efficiency engines by playing tiles to block where they can build for free, you can time when to add new terrain tiles to allow you to time when to make a mad rush for connecting temples and there are many other strategies and options in play.

RATING: 8
As a two-player game, Attika is quite good. The tension between placing your own buildings and blocking your opponent's makes for a great, quick game. With more players, there is a kingmaker problem and you have less control (i.e., someone else's mistake can cost you the game). I once rated this game quite high. While I still enjoy it, experienced players make this less a game of connection, and more an exercise in building -- which removes much of the tension.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Attika is really fun for me.  I love the chart with all of the buildings with their hierarchies.  Really, you are planning out the most efficient way to build the buildings available to you, while keeping an eye on blocking connections if you have to.  It's nice to wait and see as many buildings as possible before you build, so you can plan everything.  But you always have to go sooner than you want in order to use the natural resources on the board before others scoop them all up.  Great game.

RATING: 3
The players have relatively little control.  They can only purchase the buildings they draw and the layout of the board shoe-horns the players into placing the buildings into particular spaces for the resource benefits.  The actual decisions aren't difficult.  ("I need some cards, so I guess I'll draw cards; I'm out of buildings so I guess I'll draw tiles; My opponent's trying to connect two shrines so I guess I'd better block him.")

It feels like a derivative amalgamation of Knizia's tile-placement games and Settlers of Catan.

I'd like to try it with more players because I think what could make this game enjoyable is a more chaotic race for the best spots.  As a two-player game, it's pretty dull.

RATING: 7.5
Somewhat cut-throat game of managing random draws and building efficiency made much more interesting by fending off the threat of a instant-win for any player connecting temples.

The extra turn carrots guide building choices by rewarding certain groupings, the free builds for certain buildings adjacent to their prerequisites can be frustratingly elusive, and jugging resources printed on the board and from cards is lots of fun. Make interesting by the opportunity to keep tiles on your build sheet until they're playable.

RATING: 7
Fast, but decision-heavy Euro.  One of the best Euros of its era.  There's a delicious tension between developing your city as fast as possible to snatch up the good resource areas, and turning up tiles and getting resources to set yourself up for more efficient building later.  There's definitely an element of luck in this game, but it's just the right amount.  The expert player will usually win, but can occasionally be caught by a less experienced player with a bit of luck.  The variable map also makes for plenty of replayability.

RATING: 9
A best-of-breed eurogame.  Extremely elegant, scads of difficult decisions, yet the constraints reduce AP and downtime.  The pressure of shrine connections creates a perfect balance between forced defensive play and pure planning.  Increasing settlement costs makes defense and/or opportunism expensive, which is just right.  Amphoras are another great touch, again adding layers to the evaluation of each move, but without adding rules cruft.

Pure genius.

RATING: 6
I really like the components and feel of the gameplay pacing, but I was taken aback at the huge role of luck. If one player gets their core city as the first draw from their stack of black tiles, they have a huge advantage over someone with that starting city further down the stack.

If that can be addressed, I do like the efficiency decisions facing players, and the cascade effect of building structures for free or reduced costs if timed properly. As is, the outcome is determined by the setup of the player's tile stacks more than their decisions. Once you balance the starting tile draws, the rest of the game is quite enjoyable.

RATING: 7

There are also instances when one has to sacrifice himself by making a suboptimal move to stop someone else from taking control of important regions of the board.  Decent game, plays quick, but not enough meat.
